href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid solid #aaa;">\nSocietal components</th></tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Financial_law" title="Financial law">Financial law</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Financial_market" title="Financial market">Financial market</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Financial_market_participants" title="Financial market participants">Financial market finance</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">Banks and banking</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Financial_regulation" title="Financial regulation">Financial regulation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Clawback" title="Clawback">Clawback</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Corporate_finance" title="Template:Corporate finance"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Corporate_finance" title="Template talk:Corporate finance"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Corporate_finance&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>credit rating agency</b> (<b>CRA</b>, also called a <b>ratings service</b>) is a company that assigns <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit ratings</a>, which rate a debtor\'s ability to pay back debt by making timely interest payments and the likelihood of <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">default</a>. An agency may rate the creditworthiness of <a href="/wiki/Issuer" title="Issuer">issuers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a> obligations, of debt instruments,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and in some cases, of the servicers of the underlying debt,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> but not of individual consumers.\n</p><p>The debt instruments rated by CRAs include <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">government bonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">corporate bonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Certificate_of_deposit" title="Certificate of deposit">CDs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_bond" title="Municipal bond">municipal bonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred stock</a>, and collateralized securities, such as <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The issuers of the obligations or <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> may be companies, <a href="/wiki/Special_purpose_entity" class="mw-redirect" title="Special purpose entity">special purpose entities</a>, state or local governments, <a href="/wiki/Non-profit_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-profit organization">non-profit organizations</a>, or sovereign nations.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> A credit rating facilitates the trading of securities on a <a href="/wiki/Secondary_market" title="Secondary market">secondary market</a>. It affects the <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate" title="Interest rate">interest rate</a> that a security pays out, with higher ratings leading to lower interest rates. Individual consumers are rated for creditworthiness not by credit rating agencies but by <a href="/wiki/Credit_bureau" title="Credit bureau">credit bureaus</a> (also called consumer reporting agencies or credit reference agencies), which issue <a href="/wiki/Credit_score" title="Credit score">credit scores</a>.\n</p><p>The value of credit ratings for securities has been widely questioned. Hundreds of billions of securities that were given the agencies\' highest ratings were downgraded to junk during the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x9308">financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x9308</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLean_p.111_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean_p.111-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meltdown_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meltdown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Rating downgrades during the <a href="/wiki/European_sovereign_debt_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European sovereign debt crisis">European sovereign debt crisis</a> of 2010\xe2\x80\x9312 were blamed by EU officials for accelerating the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Credit rating is a highly concentrated industry, with the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_(credit_rating_agencies)" title="Big Three (credit rating agencies)">"Big Three" credit rating agencies</a> controlling approximately 95% of the ratings business.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moody%27s_Investors_Service" title="Moody&#39;s Investors Service">Moody\'s Investors Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s#Credit_ratings" title="Standard &amp; Poor&#39;s">Standard &amp; Poor\'s</a> (S&amp;P) together control 80% of the global market, and <a href="/wiki/Fitch_Ratings" title="Fitch Ratings">Fitch Ratings</a> controls a further 15%.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When the United States began to expand to the west and other parts of the country, so did the distance of businesses to their customers. When businesses were close to those who purchased goods or services from them, it was easy for the merchants to extend credit to them, due to their proximity and the fact that merchants knew their customers personally and knew whether or not they would be able to pay them back. As trading distances increased, merchants no longer personally knew their customers and became leery of extending credit to people who they did not know in fear of them not being able to pay them back. Business owners\' hesitation to extend credit to new customers led to the birth of the credit reporting industry.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mercantile credit agencies\xe2\x80\x94the precursors of today\'s rating agencies\xe2\x80\x94were established in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">financial crisis of 1837</a>. These agencies rated the ability of merchants to pay their debts and consolidated these ratings in published guides.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The first such agency was established in 1841 by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Tappan" title="Lewis Tappan">Lewis Tappan</a> in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Langhor_09_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langhor_09-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It was subsequently acquired by Robert Dun, who published its first ratings guide in 1859. Another early agency, John Bradstreet, formed in 1849 and published a ratings guide in 1857.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Credit rating agencies originated in the United States in the early 1900s, when ratings began to be applied to securities, specifically those related to the railroad bond market.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, the construction of extensive railroad systems had led to the development of corporate bond issues to finance them, and therefore a bond market several times larger than in other countries. The bond markets in the Netherlands and Britain had been established longer but tended to be small, and revolved around sovereign governments that were trusted to honor their debts.<sup id="cite_ref-Sylla_01_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sylla_01-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Companies were founded to provide investors with financial information on the growing railroad industry, including <a href="/wiki/Henry_Varnum_Poor" title="Henry Varnum Poor">Henry Varnum Poor</a>\'s publishing company, which produced a publication compiling financial data about the railroad and canal industries.<sup id="cite_ref-Karp_11_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karp_11-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1907" title="Panic of 1907">1907 financial crisis</a>, demand rose for such independent market information, in particular for independent analyses of bond creditworthiness.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1909, financial analyst <a href="/wiki/John_Moody_(financial_analyst)" title="John Moody (financial analyst)">John Moody</a> issued a publication focused solely on railroad bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JEP_Spring_10_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEP_Spring_10-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yasuyuki_Litan_06_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasuyuki_Litan_06-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> His ratings became the first to be published widely in an accessible format,<sup id="cite_ref-Sylla_01_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sylla_01-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and his company was the first to charge subscription fees to investors.<sup id="cite_ref-Yasuyuki_Litan_06_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasuyuki_Litan_06-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1913, the ratings publication by Moody\'s underwent two significant changes: it expanded its focus to include industrial firms and utilities, and it began to use a letter-rating system. For the first time, public securities were rated using a system borrowed from the mercantile credit rating agencies, using letters to indicate their creditworthiness.<sup id="cite_ref-Moody&#39;s_History_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moody&#39;s_History-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In the next few years, antecedents of the "<a href="/wiki/Big_Three_(credit_rating_agencies)" title="Big Three (credit rating agencies)">Big Three</a>" credit rating agencies were established. Poor\'s Publishing Company began issuing ratings in 1916, Standard Statistics Company in 1922,<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and the Fitch Publishing Company in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-JEP_Spring_10_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEP_Spring_10-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Depression_era">Post-Depression era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-Depression era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the United States, the rating industry grew and consolidated rapidly following the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Legislation" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass\xe2\x80\x93Steagall Legislation">Glass-Steagall act</a> of 1933 and the separation of the securities business from banking.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-26_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-26-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As the market grew beyond that of traditional investment banking institutions, new investors again called for increased transparency, leading to the passage of new, mandatory disclosure laws for issuers, and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC).<sup id="cite_ref-Sylla_01_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sylla_01-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 1936, regulation was introduced to prohibit banks from investing in bonds determined by "recognized rating manuals" (the forerunners of credit rating agencies) to be "speculative investment securities" ("junk bonds", in modern terminology). US banks were permitted to hold only "investment grade" bonds, and it was the ratings of Fitch, Moody\'s, Poor\'s, and Standard that legally determined which bonds were which. State insurance regulators approved similar requirements in the following decades.<sup id="cite_ref-JEP_Spring_10_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEP_Spring_10-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1930 to 1980, the bonds and ratings of them were primarily relegated to American municipalities and American blue chip industrial firms.<sup id="cite_ref-Toffler_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toffler-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> International "sovereign bond" rating shrivelled during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> to a handful of the most creditworthy countries,<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-sovereign_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-sovereign-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> after a number of defaults of bonds issued by governments such as Germany\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-Toffler_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toffler-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 1960s and 1970s, ratings were extended to <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deposit_account" title="Deposit account">bank deposits</a>. Also during that time, major agencies changed their business model by beginning to charge bond issuers as well as investors.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The reasons for this change included a growing <a href="/wiki/Free_rider_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Free rider problem">free rider problem</a> related to the increasing availability of inexpensive <a href="/wiki/Photocopier" title="Photocopier">photocopy machines</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wash_Post_Jan_02_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wash_Post_Jan_02-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and the increased complexity of the financial markets.<sup id="cite_ref-AEI_Jan_09_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEI_Jan_09-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rating agencies added levels of gradation to their rating systems. In 1973, Fitch added plus and minus symbols to its existing letter-rating system. The following year, Standard and Poor\'s did the same, and Moody\'s began using numbers for the same purpose in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_bond_market">Growth of bond market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Growth of bond market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The end of the <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">Bretton Woods system</a> in 1971 led to the liberalization of financial regulations and the global expansion of capital markets in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1975, SEC rules began explicitly referencing credit ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox_07_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox_07-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> For example, the commission changed its minimum capital requirements for <a href="/wiki/Broker-dealer" title="Broker-dealer">broker-dealers</a>, allowing smaller reserves for higher-rated bonds; the rating would be done by "nationally recognized statistical ratings organizations" (NRSROs). This referred to the "Big Three",<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94-8_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> but in time ten agencies (later six, due to consolidation) were identified by the SEC as NRSROs.<sup id="cite_ref-JEP_Spring_10_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEP_Spring_10-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SEC_NRSRO_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEC_NRSRO-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rating agencies also grew in size and profitability as the number of issuers accessing the debt markets grew exponentially, both in the United States and abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_09_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke_09-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> By 2009 the worldwide bond market (total debt outstanding) reached an estimated $82.2 trillion, in 2009 dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-World_bond_market_debt_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_bond_market_debt-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1980s.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s\xe2\x80\x93present">1980s\xe2\x80\x93present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1980s\xe2\x80\x93present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Two economic trends of the 1980s and 90s that brought significant expansion for the global capital market were<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>the move away from "intermediated" financing (bank loans) toward cheaper and longer-term "disintermediated" financing (tradable bonds and other fixed income securities),<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05_54-56_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05_54-56-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and</li>\n<li>the global move away from state intervention and state-led industrial adjustment toward <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a> based on (among other things) global capital markets and arms-length relations between government and industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05_57-59_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05_57-59-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>More debt securities meant more business for the Big Three agencies, which many investors depended on to judge the securities of the capital market.<sup id="cite_ref-Yasuyuki_Litan_06_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasuyuki_Litan_06-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> US government regulators also depended on the rating agencies; they allowed pension funds and money market funds to purchase only securities rated above certain levels.<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-119_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-119-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A market for <a href="/wiki/High-yield_debt" title="High-yield debt">low-rated, high-yield "junk" bonds</a> blossomed in the late 1970s, expanding securities financing to firms other than a few large, established <a href="/wiki/Blue_chip_(stock_market)" title="Blue chip (stock market)">blue chip</a> corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05_57-59_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05_57-59-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Rating agencies also began to apply their ratings beyond bonds to counterparty risks, the performance risk of mortgage servicers, and the price volatility of mutual funds and mortgage-backed securities.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Ratings were increasingly used in most developed countries\' financial markets and in the "<a href="/wiki/Emerging_markets" class="mw-redirect" title="Emerging markets">emerging markets</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing world</a>. Moody\'s and S&amp;P opened offices Europe, Japan, and particularly emerging markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Non-American agencies also developed outside of the United States. Along with the largest US raters, one British, two Canadian and three Japanese firms were listed among the world\'s "most influential" rating agencies in the early 1990s by the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> publication <i>Credit Ratings International</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94-2_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-2-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Structured_finance" title="Structured finance">Structured finance</a> was another growth area of growth. The "financial engineering" of the new "private-label" <a href="/wiki/Asset-backed_security" title="Asset-backed security">asset-backed securities</a>\xe2\x80\x94such as subprime <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> (MBS), <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDO), "<a href="/wiki/CDO-Squared" title="CDO-Squared">CDO-Squared</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDOs</a>"\xe2\x80\x94made them "harder to understand and to price" and became a <a href="/wiki/Profit_center" title="Profit center">profit center</a> for rating agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-big_3_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-big_3-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> By 2006, Moody\'s earned $881 million in revenue from structured finance.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_SF_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy_SF-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> By December 2008, there were over $11 trillion structured finance debt securities outstanding in the US bond market.<sup id="cite_ref-nber_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nber-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Big Three issued 97%\xe2\x80\x9398% of all credit ratings in the United States<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and roughly 95% worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-Cfr_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cfr-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> giving them considerable pricing power.<sup id="cite_ref-Flawed2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flawed2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This and credit market expansion brought them profit margins of around 50% from 2004 through 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Flawed_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flawed-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the influence and profitability of CRAs expanded, so did scrutiny and concern about their performance and alleged illegal practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-154_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-154-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 1996 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">US Department of Justice</a> launched an investigation into possible improper pressuring of issuers by Moody\'s in order to win business.<sup id="cite_ref-Tulsa_March_1996_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tulsa_March_1996-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-152_pressure_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-152_pressure-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Agencies were subjected to dozens of lawsuits by investors complaining of inaccurate ratings<sup id="cite_ref-dozens_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dozens-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Utley_96_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utley_96-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> following the <a href="/wiki/Enron_scandal" title="Enron scandal">collapse of Enron</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and especially after the US <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a> and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s financial crisis">late-2000s financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CGFS_May_08_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CGFS_May_08-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gallu_11_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallu_11-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> During that debacle, 73%\xe2\x80\x94over $800 billion worth<sup id="cite_ref-controversy-83_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-83-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94of all mortgage-backed securities that one credit rating agency (Moody\'s) had rated triple-A in 2006 were downgraded to junk status two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy-83_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-83-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-122_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-122-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nDowngrades of European and US sovereign debt were also criticized. In August 2011, S&amp;P downgraded the long-held triple-A rating of US securities.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Since the spring of 2010, </p><blockquote><p>one or more of the Big Three relegated Greece, Portugal, and Ireland to "<a href="/wiki/High-yield_debt" title="High-yield debt">junk</a>" status&#8212;a move that many EU officials say has accelerated a burgeoning <a href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">European sovereign-debt crisis</a>. In January 2012, amid continued <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a> instability, S&amp;P downgraded nine eurozone countries, stripping France and Austria of their <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating" title="Bond credit rating">triple-A ratings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-controversy_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_capital_markets">Role in capital markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Role in capital markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Credit rating agencies assess the relative <a href="/wiki/Credit_risk" title="Credit risk">credit risk</a> of specific <a href="/wiki/Debt_securities" class="mw-redirect" title="Debt securities">debt securities</a> or <a href="/wiki/Structured_finance" title="Structured finance">structured finance instruments</a> and borrowing entities (<a href="/wiki/Issuer" title="Issuer">issuers</a> of debt),<sup id="cite_ref-Essvale_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Essvale-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and in some cases the <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating#Sovereign_credit_ratings" title="Credit rating">creditworthiness</a> of governments and their <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">securities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crockett_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crockett-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> By serving as information <a href="/wiki/Intermediary" title="Intermediary">intermediaries</a>, CRAs theoretically reduce information costs, increase the pool of potential borrowers, and promote <a href="/wiki/Market_liquidity" title="Market liquidity">liquid markets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heritier_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heritier-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naciri_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naciri-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> These functions may increase the supply of available <a href="/wiki/Risk_capital" class="mw-redirect" title="Risk capital">risk capital</a> in the market and promote economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Essvale_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Essvale-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naciri_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naciri-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratings_use_in_bond_market">Ratings use in bond market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ratings use in bond market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">Credit rating</a></div>\n<p>Credit rating agencies provide assessments about the creditworthiness of <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a> issued by <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">governments</a>, and packagers of <a href="/wiki/Asset-backed_security" title="Asset-backed security">asset-backed securities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WhiteAltman_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WhiteAltman-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stowell2012_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stowell2012-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In market practice, a significant bond issuance generally has a rating from one or two of the Big Three agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Maclean_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maclean-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>CRAs theoretically provide investors with an independent evaluation and assessment of <a href="/wiki/Debt_securities#Debt" class="mw-redirect" title="Debt securities">debt securities</a>\' creditworthiness.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR-quote_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR-quote-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> However, in recent decades the paying customers of CRAs have primarily not been issuers of securities but buyers, raising the issue of conflict of interest (see below).<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-pay_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-pay-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition, rating agencies have been liable\xe2\x80\x94at least in US courts\xe2\x80\x94for any losses incurred by the inaccuracy of their ratings only if it is proven that they knew the ratings were false or exhibited "reckless disregard for the truth".<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-119_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-119-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-buttonwood_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buttonwood-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, ratings are simply an expression of the agencies\' informed opinions,<sup id="cite_ref-CaouetteAltman_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CaouetteAltman-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> protected as "free speech" under the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AshbyJones_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AshbyJones-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EconomistFreeSpeech_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconomistFreeSpeech-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> As one rating agency disclaimer read: \n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The ratings ... are and must be construed solely as, statements of opinion and not statements of fact or recommendations to purchase, sell, or hold any securities.<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> Under an amendment to the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" title="Dodd\xe2\x80\x93Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd-Frank Act</a>, this protection has been removed, but how the law will be implemented remains to be determined by rules made by the SEC and decisions by courts.<sup id="cite_ref-Madura49_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madura49-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lippert_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippert-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haar_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haar-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To determine a bond\'s <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">rating</a>, a credit rating agency analyzes the accounts of the issuer and the legal agreements attached to the bond<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yescombe_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yescombe-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> to produce what is effectively a forecast of the bond\'s chance of <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">default</a>, expected loss, or a similar metric.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The metrics vary somewhat between the agencies. S&amp;P\'s ratings reflect default probability, while ratings by Moody\'s reflect expected investor losses in the case of default.<sup id="cite_ref-Salmon_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salmon-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samuelson_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samuelson-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> For corporate obligations, Fitch\'s ratings incorporate a measure of investor loss in the event of default, but its ratings on structured, project, and public finance obligations narrowly measure default risk.<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The process and criteria for rating a convertible bond are similar, although different enough that bonds and convertible bonds issued by the same entity may still receive different ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-Schoutens_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoutens-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Some bank loans may receive ratings to assist in wider <a href="/wiki/Syndicated_loan" title="Syndicated loan">syndication</a> and attract institutional investors.<sup id="cite_ref-Yescombe_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yescombe-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relative risks\xe2\x80\x94the rating grades\xe2\x80\x94are usually expressed through some variation of an alphabetical combination of lower- and uppercase letters, with either plus or minus signs or numbers added to further fine-tune the rating.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR8889_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR8889-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levin27_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin27-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating#Credit_rating_tiers" title="Bond credit rating">Bond credit rating \xc2\xa7&#160;Credit rating tiers</a></div> \n<p>Fitch and S&amp;P use (from the most creditworthy to the least) AAA, AA, A, and BBB for investment-grade long-term credit risk and BB, CCC, CC, C, and D for "speculative" long-term credit risk. Moody\'s long-term designators are Aaa, Aa, A, and Baa for investment grade and Ba, B, Caa, Ca, and C for speculative grade.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR8889_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR8889-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levin27_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin27-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Fitch and S&amp;P use pluses and minuses (e.g., AA+ and AA-), and Moody\'s uses numbers (e.g., Aa1 and Aa3) to add further gradations.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR8889_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR8889-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levin27_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin27-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float:right">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="3">Estimated spreads and <br />default rates by rating grade\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Rating\n</th>\n<th>Basis <br />point <br />spread<br /><sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94-10_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-10-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-altman_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-altman-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th>Default<br />rate<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_p24_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor_p24-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>AAA/Aaa</td>\n<td>43</td>\n<td>0.18%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>AA/Aa2</td>\n<td>73</td>\n<td>0.28%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>A</td>\n<td>99</td>\n<td>n/a\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BBB/Baa2</td>\n<td>166</td>\n<td>2.11%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>BB/Ba2</td>\n<td>299</td>\n<td>8.82%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>B/B2</td>\n<td>404</td>\n<td>31.24%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>CCC</td>\n<td>724</td>\n<td>n/a\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="3"><small>Sources: Basis spread is <br />between US treasuries<br />and rated bonds<br />over a 16-year period;<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94-8_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-altman_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-altman-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><br /> Default rate over a <br />5-year period, from a study<br />by Moody\'s investment service<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_p24_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor_p24-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></small>\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Agencies do not attach a hard number of <a href="/wiki/Probability_of_default" title="Probability of default">probability of default</a> to each grade, preferring descriptive definitions, such as "the obligor\'s capacity to meet its financial commitment on the obligation is extremely strong," (from a Standard and Poor\'s definition of a AAA-rated bond) or "less vulnerable to non-payment than other speculative issues" (for a BB-rated bond).<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05-36_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-36-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> However, some studies have estimated the average risk and reward of bonds by rating. One study by Moody\'s<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_p24_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor_p24-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> claimed that over a "5-year time horizon", bonds that were given its highest rating (Aaa) had a "cumulative default rate" of just 0.18%, the next highest (Aa2) 0.28%, the next (Baa2) 2.11%, 8.82% for the next (Ba2), and 31.24% for the lowest it studied (B2). (See "Default rate" in "Estimated spreads and default rates by rating grade" table to right.) Over a longer time horizon, it stated, "the order is by and large, but not exactly, preserved".<sup id="cite_ref-defaults_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-defaults-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another study in the <i>Journal of Finance</i> calculated the additional interest rate or "spread" that corporate bonds pay over that of "riskless" US Treasury bonds, according to the bonds rating. (See "Basis point spread" in the table to right.) Looking at rated bonds from 1973 through 1989, the authors found a AAA-rated bond paid only 43 "<a href="/wiki/Basis_points" class="mw-redirect" title="Basis points">basis points</a>" (or 43/100ths of a percentage point) more than a Treasury bond (so that it would yield 3.43% if the Treasury bond yielded 3.00%). A CCC-rated "junk" (or speculative) bond, on the other hand, paid over 4% more than a Treasury bond on average (7.04% if the Treasury bond yielded 3.00%) over that period.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_94-8_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_94-8-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-altman_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-altman-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The market also follows the benefits from ratings that result from government regulations (see <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agency#Use_by_government_regulators" title="Credit rating agency">below</a>), which often prohibit financial institutions from purchasing securities rated below a certain level. For example, in the United States, in accordance with two 1989 regulations, <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> are prohibited from investing in asset-backed securities rated below A,<sup id="cite_ref-Sicilia_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sicilia-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_association" title="Savings and loan association">savings and loan associations</a> from investing in securities rated below BBB.<sup id="cite_ref-masters-43_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masters-43-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>CRAs provide "surveillance" (ongoing review of securities after their initial rating) and may change a security\'s rating if they feel its creditworthiness has changed. CRAs typically signal in advance their intention to consider rating changes.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR8889_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR8889-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arezki5_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arezki5-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Fitch, Moody\'s, and S&amp;P all use negative "outlook" notifications to indicate the potential for a downgrade within the next two years (one year in the case of speculative-grade credits). Negative "watch" notifications are used to indicate that a downgrade is likely within the next 90 days.<sup id="cite_ref-GFSR8889_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GFSR8889-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arezki5_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arezki5-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accuracy_and_responsiveness">Accuracy and responsiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Accuracy and responsiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agencies_and_the_subprime_crisis" title="Credit rating agencies and the subprime crisis">Credit rating agencies and the subprime crisis</a></div>\n<p>Critics maintain that this rating, outlooking, and watching of securities has not worked nearly as smoothly as agencies suggest. They point to near-defaults, defaults, and financial disasters not detected by the rating agencies\' post-issuance surveillance, or ratings of troubled <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">debt securities</a> not downgraded until just before (or even after) bankruptcy.<sup id="cite_ref-Maclean2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maclean2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> These include the 1970 <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company#Bankruptcy" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central bankruptcy</a>, the 1975 <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_fiscal_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="New York City fiscal crisis">New York City fiscal crisis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California#History" title="Orange County, California">1994 Orange County default</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Russian_financial_crisis" title="1998 Russian financial crisis">Russian</a> financial crises, the 1998 collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Long-Term_Capital_Management" title="Long-Term Capital Management">Long-Term Capital Management</a> hedge fund, the 2001 <a href="/wiki/Enron_scandal" title="Enron scandal">Enron</a> and <a href="/wiki/WorldCom#Bankruptcy" class="mw-redirect" title="WorldCom">WorldCom</a> bankruptcies, and especially the 2007\xe2\x80\x938 <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maclean2_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maclean2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLean113_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean113-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-White_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stowell146_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stowell146-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levin_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2001 <a href="/wiki/Enron#2001_Accounting_scandals" title="Enron">Enron accounting scandal</a>, the company\'s ratings remained at investment grade until four days before bankruptcy\xe2\x80\x94though Enron\'s stock had been in sharp decline for several months<sup id="cite_ref-enron1_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enron1-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enron2_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enron2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94when "the outlines of its fraudulent practices" were first revealed.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Critics complained that "not a single analyst at either Moody\'s of S&amp;P lost his job as a result of missing the Enron fraud" and "management stayed the same".<sup id="cite_ref-McLean-p120_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-p120-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> During the subprime crisis, when hundreds of billion of dollars\' worth<sup id="cite_ref-controversy-83_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-83-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> of triple-A-rated mortgage-backed securities were abruptly downgraded from triple-A to "junk" status within two years of issue,<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-122_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-122-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> the CRAs\' ratings were characterized by critics as "catastrophically misleading"<sup id="cite_ref-casey_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casey-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and "provided little or no value".<sup id="cite_ref-Lippert_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippert-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Ratings of preferred stocks also fared poorly. Despite over a year of rising mortgage deliquencies,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Moody\'s continued to rate <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie Mac</a>\'s preferred stock triple-A until mid-2008, when it was downgraded to one tick above the <a href="/wiki/High-yield_debt" title="High-yield debt">junk bond</a> level.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-20080922_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20080922-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LewsiBigShort_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LewsiBigShort-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Some empirical studies have also found that rather than a downgrade lowering the market price and raising the interest rates of corporate bonds, the cause and effect are reversed. Expanding yield spreads (i.e., declining value and quality) of corporate bonds precedes downgrades by agencies, suggesting it is the market that alerts the CRAs of trouble and not vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-Kliger_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kliger-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Galil_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galil-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2018, an investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Securities_and_Investments_Commission" title="Australian Securities and Investments Commission">Australian Securities and Investments Commission</a> found a serious lack of detail and rigour in many of the ratings issued by agencies. ASIC examined six agencies, including the Australian arms of Fitch, Moody\'s and S&amp;P Global Ratings (the other agencies were Best Asia-Pacific, Australia Ratings and Equifax Australia). It said agencies had often paid lip service to compliance. In one case, an agency had issued an annual compliance report only a single page in length, with scant discussion of methodology. In another case, a chief executive officer of a company had signed off on a report as though a board member. Also, overseas staff of ratings agencies had assigned credit ratings despite lacking the necessary accreditation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanations_of_flaws">Explanations of flaws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Explanations of flaws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Defenders of credit rating agencies complain of the market\'s lack of appreciation. Argues Robert Clow, "When a company or sovereign nation pays its debt on time, the market barely takes momentary notice ... but let a country or corporation unexpectedly miss a payment or threaten default, and bondholders, lawyers and even regulators are quick to rush the field to protest the credit analyst\'s lapse."<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_05_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_05-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Others say that bonds assigned a low credit rating by rating agencies have been shown to default more frequently than bonds that receive a high credit rating, suggesting that ratings still serve as a useful indicator of credit risk.<sup id="cite_ref-Madura49_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madura49-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of explanations of the rating agencies\' inaccurate ratings and forecasts have been offered, especially in the wake of the subprime crisis:<sup id="cite_ref-White_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levin_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<ul><li>The methodologies employed by agencies to rate and monitor securities may be inherently flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-Utzig_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utzig-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> For instance, a 2008 report by the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Stability_Forum" title="Financial Stability Forum">Financial Stability Forum</a> singled out methodological shortcomings\xe2\x80\x94especially inadequate historical data\xe2\x80\x94as a contributing cause in the underestimating of the risk in structured finance products by the CRAs before the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FSFreport_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FSFreport-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The ratings process relies on subjective judgments. This means that governments, for example, that are being rated can often inform and influence credit rating analysts during the review process<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The rating agencies\' interest in pleasing the issuers of securities, who are their <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agency#Business_models" title="Credit rating agency">paying customers</a> and benefit from high ratings, creates a <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict</a> with their interest in providing accurate ratings of securities for investors buying the securities.<sup id="cite_ref-Khanzada567_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khanzada567-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Issuers of securities benefit from higher ratings in that many of their customers\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Retail_banking" title="Retail banking">retail banks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Money_market_fund" title="Money market fund">money market funds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insurance#Insurance_companies" title="Insurance">insurance companies</a>\xe2\x80\x94are prohibited by law or otherwise restrained from buying securities below a certain rating.<sup id="cite_ref-masters-43_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masters-43-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The rating agencies may have been significantly understaffed during the subprime boom and thus unable to properly assess every debt instrument.<sup id="cite_ref-McLean123_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean123-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Agency analysts may be underpaid relative to similar positions at <a href="/wiki/Investment_banks" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment banks">investment banks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> firms, resulting in a migration of credit rating analysts and the analysts\' inside knowledge of rating procedures to higher-paying jobs at the banks and firms that issue the securities being rated, and thereby facilitating the manipulation of ratings by issuers.<sup id="cite_ref-LewsiBigShort_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LewsiBigShort-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The functional use of ratings as regulatory mechanisms may inflate their reputation for accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-PartnoySiskel_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PartnoySiskel-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Excessive_power">Excessive power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Excessive power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Conversely, the complaint has been made that agencies have too much power over issuers and that downgrades can even force troubled companies into bankruptcy. The lowering of a credit score by a CRA can create a <a href="/wiki/Vicious_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Vicious cycle">vicious cycle</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Self-fulfilling_prophecy" title="Self-fulfilling prophecy">self-fulfilling prophecy</a>: not only do interest rates on securities rise, but other contracts with financial institutions may also be affected adversely, causing an increase in financing costs and an ensuing decrease in creditworthiness. Large loans to companies often contain a clause that makes the loan due in full if the company\'s credit rating is lowered beyond a certain point (usually from investment grade to "speculative"). The purpose of these "ratings triggers" is to ensure that the loan-making bank is able to lay claim to a weak company\'s assets before the company declares <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy" title="Bankruptcy">bankruptcy</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Receivership" title="Receivership">receiver</a> is appointed to divide up the claims against the company. The effect of such ratings triggers, however, can be devastating: under a worst-case scenario, once the company\'s debt is downgraded by a CRA, the company\'s loans become due in full; if the company is incapable of paying all of these loans in full at once, it is forced into bankruptcy (a so-called <a href="/wiki/Death_spiral_financing" title="Death spiral financing">death spiral</a>). These ratings triggers were instrumental in the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Enron" title="Enron">Enron</a>. Since that time, major agencies have put extra effort into detecting them and discouraging their use, and the US <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a> requires that public companies in the United States disclose their existence.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_laws">Reform laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reform laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The 2010 <a href="/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" title="Dodd\xe2\x80\x93Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd\xe2\x80\x93Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</a><sup id="cite_ref-Dodd-Frank_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dodd-Frank-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> mandated improvements to the regulation of credit rating agencies and addressed several issues relating to the accuracy of credit ratings specifically.<sup id="cite_ref-Madura49_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madura49-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Under Dodd-Frank rules, agencies must publicly disclose how their ratings have performed over time and must provide additional information in their analyses so investors can make better decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-Madura49_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madura49-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> An amendment to the act also specifies that ratings are not protected by the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment" class="mw-redirect" title="First Amendment">First Amendment</a> as <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Free speech">free speech</a> but are "fundamentally commercial in character and should be subject to the same standards of liability and oversight as apply to auditors, securities analysts and investment bankers."<sup id="cite_ref-Lippert_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippert-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Madura49_65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madura49-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Implementation of this amendment has proven difficult due to conflict between the SEC and the rating agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haar_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haar-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Economist</i> magazine credits the free speech defence at least in part for the fact that "41 legal actions targeting S&amp;P have been dropped or dismissed" since the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-econ2_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ2-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, there is no specific legislation governing contracts between issuers and credit rating agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Haar_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haar-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> General rules of contract law apply in full, although it is difficult to hold agencies liable for breach of contract.<sup id="cite_ref-Haar_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haar-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, an Australian federal court held <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor&#39;s">Standard &amp; Poor\'s</a> liable for inaccurate ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-Haar_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haar-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratings_use_in_structured_finance">Ratings use in structured finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ratings use in structured finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Structured_finance" title="Structured finance">Structured finance</a></div>\n<p>Credit rating agencies play a key role in <a href="/wiki/Structured_finance" title="Structured finance">structured financial transactions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Asset-backed_security" title="Asset-backed security">asset-backed securities</a> (ABS), <a href="/wiki/Residential_mortgage-backed_security" title="Residential mortgage-backed security">residential mortgage-backed securities</a> (RMBS), <a href="/wiki/Commercial_mortgage-backed_security" title="Commercial mortgage-backed security">commercial mortgage-backed securities</a> (CMBS), <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDOs), "<a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDOs</a>", or <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fabozzi_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fabozzi-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Credit ratings for structured finance instruments may be distinguished from ratings for other debt securities in several important ways.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>These securities are more complex and an accurate prognoses of repayment more difficult than with other debt ratings.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Qiao_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qiao-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> This is because they are formed by pooling debt \xe2\x80\x94 usually consumer credit assets, such as mortgages, credit card or auto loans \xe2\x80\x94 and structured by "slicing" the pool into multiple "<a href="/wiki/Tranche" title="Tranche">tranches</a>", each with a different priority of payment. Tranches are often likened to buckets capturing cascading water, where the water of monthly or quarterly repayment flows down to the next bucket (tranche) only if the one above has been filled with its full share and is overflowing. The higher-up the bucket in the income stream, the lower its risk, the higher its credit rating, and lower its interest payment.<sup id="cite_ref-Upstart_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Upstart-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> This means the higher-level tranches have more credit worthiness than would a conventional unstructured, untranched bond with the same repayment income stream, and allows rating agencies to rate the tranches triple A or other high grades. Such securities are then eligible for purchase by <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Money_market_fund" title="Money market fund">money market funds</a> restricted to higher-rated debt, and for use by banks wanting to reduce costly capital requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CovalHBR_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CovalHBR-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Telpner_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telpner-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>CRAs are not only paid for giving ratings to structured securities, but may be paid for advice on how to structure tranches<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and sometimes the underlying assets that secure the debt<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StowellStructured_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StowellStructured-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> to achieve ratings the issuer desires. This involves back and forth interaction and analysis between the sponsor of the trust that issues the security and the rating agency.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> During this process, the sponsor may submit proposed structures to the agency for analysis and feedback until the sponsor is satisfied with the ratings of the different <a href="/wiki/Tranche" title="Tranche">tranches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StowellStructured_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StowellStructured-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Credit rating agencies employ varying methodologies to rate structured finance products, but generally focus on the type of pool of financial assets underlying the security and the proposed capital structure of the trust.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> This approach often involves a <a href="/wiki/Numerical_data" class="mw-redirect" title="Numerical data">quantitative</a> assessment in accordance with mathematical models, and may thus introduce a degree of <a href="/wiki/Model_risk" title="Model risk">model risk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haslett434_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haslett434-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> However, bank models of risk assessment have proven less reliable than credit rating agency models, even in the base of large banks with sophisticated risk management procedures.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBankStateFinance_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBankStateFinance-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Aside from investors mentioned above\xe2\x80\x94who are subject to ratings-based constraints in buying securities\xe2\x80\x94some investors simply prefer that a structured finance product be rated by a credit rating agency.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> And not all structured finance products receive a credit rating agency rating.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Ratings for complicated or risky CDOs are unusual and some issuers create structured products relying solely on internal analytics to assess credit risk.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subprime_mortgage_boom_and_crisis">Subprime mortgage boom and crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subprime mortgage boom and crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agencies_and_the_subprime_crisis" title="Credit rating agencies and the subprime crisis">Credit rating agencies and the subprime crisis</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> has described the Big Three rating agencies as "key players in the process" of mortgage <a href="/wiki/Securitization" title="Securitization">securitization</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-big_3_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-big_3-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> providing reassurance of the soundness of the securities to money manager investors with "no history in the mortgage business".<sup id="cite_ref-Callahan_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Callahan-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Credit rating agencies began issuing ratings for mortgage-backed securities (MBS) in the mid-1970s. In subsequent years, the ratings were applied to securities backed by other types of assets.<sup id="cite_ref-IOSCO_122-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOSCO-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> During the first years of the twenty-first century, demand for highly rated fixed income securities was high.<sup id="cite_ref-Giant_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giant-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>  Growth was particularly strong and profitable in the structured finance industry during the 2001-2006 subprime mortgage boom, and business with finance industry accounted for almost all of the revenue growth at at least one of the CRAs (Moody\'s).<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 2000 to 2007, Moody\'s rated nearly 45,000 mortgage-related securities as triple-A. In contrast only six (private sector) companies in the United States were given that top rating.<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR45000_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR45000-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rating agencies were even more important in rating <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDOs). These securities mortgage/asset backed security tranches lower in the "waterfall" of repayment that could not be rated triple-A, but for whom buyers had to found or the rest of the pool of mortgages and other assets could not be securitized. Rating agencies solved the problem by rating 70%<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> to 80%<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> \nof the CDO tranches triple-A. Still another innovative structured product most of whose tranches were also given high ratings was the "<a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDO</a>". Cheaper and easier to create than ordinary "cash" CDOs, they paid insurance premium-like payments from <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swap</a> "insurance", instead of interest and principal payments from house mortgages. If the insured or "referenced" CDOs defaulted, investors lost their investment, which was paid out much like an insurance claim.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_of_interest">Conflict of interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Conflict of interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>However when it was discovered that the mortgages had been sold to buyers who could not pay them, massive numbers of securities were downgraded, the securitization "seized up" and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> ensued.<sup id="cite_ref-Search_Site_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Search_Site-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brookings.edu_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brookings.edu-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Critics blamed this underestimation of the risk of the securities on the conflict between two interests the CRAs have\xe2\x80\x94rating securities accurately, and serving their customers, the security issuers<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> who need high ratings to sell to investors subject to ratings-based constraints, such as <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life_insurance" title="Life insurance">life insurance companies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CovalHBR_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CovalHBR-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Telpner_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telpner-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\nWhile this conflict had existed for years, the combination of CRA focus on market share and earnings growth,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  the importance of structured finance to CRA profits,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> and pressure from issuers who began to `shop around` for the best ratings brought the conflict to a head between 2000 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shopping_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shopping-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-p210_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p210-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A small number of arrangers of structured finance products\xe2\x80\x94primarily <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banks</a>\xe2\x80\x94drive a large amount of business to the ratings agencies, and thus have a much greater potential to exert undue influence on a rating agency than a single corporate debt issuer.<sup id="cite_ref-CaprioCOI_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CaprioCOI-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2013 <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Finance_Institute" title="Swiss Finance Institute">Swiss Finance Institute</a> study of structured debt ratings from <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s#Credit_ratings" title="Standard &amp; Poor&#39;s">S&amp;P</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moody%27s_Investors_Service" title="Moody&#39;s Investors Service">Moody\'s</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fitch_Ratings" title="Fitch Ratings">Fitch</a> found that agencies provide better ratings for the structured products of issuers that provide them with more overall bilateral rating business.<sup id="cite_ref-SDR_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SDR-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EfingHauVOX_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EfingHauVOX-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> This effect was found to be particularly pronounced in the run-up to the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SDR_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SDR-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EfingHauVOX_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EfingHauVOX-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Alternative accounts of the agencies\' inaccurate ratings before the crisis downplay the conflict of interest factor and focus instead on the agencies\' overconfidence in rating securities, which stemmed from faith in their methodologies and past successes with subprime securitizations.<sup id="cite_ref-Hill_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hill-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katz_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katz-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Global financial crisis">global financial crisis</a>, various legal requirements were introduced to increase the transparency of structured finance ratings. The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> now requires credit rating agencies to use an additional symbol with ratings for structured finance instruments in order to distinguish them from other rating categories.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio382_116-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio382-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratings_use_in_sovereign_debt">Ratings use in sovereign debt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_rating_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ratings use in sovereign debt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_credit_rating" class="mw-redirect" title="Sovereign credit rating">Sovereign credit rating</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_credit_rating" title="List of countries by credit rating">List of countries by credit rating</a></div>\n<p>Credit rating agencies also issue credit ratings for <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> borrowers, including national governments, states, <a href="/wiki/Municipalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipalities">municipalities</a>, and sovereign-supported international entities.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio383_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio383-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Sovereign borrowers are the largest debt borrowers in many financial markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Caprio383_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caprio383-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Governments from both advanced economies and emerging markets borrow money by issuing government bonds and selling them to private investors, either overseas or domestically.<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Governments from emerging and developing markets may also choose to borrow from other governments and international organizations, such as the <a
